        <title>Genome sequence of the model rice variety KitaakeX | BMC Genomics | Full Text</title>

        

    <meta name="citation_abstract" content="The availability of thousands of complete rice genome sequences from diverse varieties and accessions has laid the foundation for in-depth exploration of the rice genome. One drawback to these collections is that most of these rice varieties have long life cycles, and/or low transformation efficiencies, which limits their usefulness as model organisms for functional genomics studies. In contrast, the rice variety Kitaake has a rapid life cycle (9&#8201;weeks seed to seed) and is easy to transform and propagate. For these reasons, Kitaake has emerged as a model for studies of diverse monocotyledonous species. Here, we report the de novo genome sequencing and analysis of Oryza sativa ssp. japonica variety KitaakeX, a Kitaake plant carrying the rice XA21 immune receptor. Our KitaakeX sequence assembly contains 377.6&#8201;Mb, consisting of 33 scaffolds (476 contigs) with a contig N50 of 1.4&#8201;Mb. Complementing the assembly are detailed gene annotations of 35,594 protein coding genes. We identified 331,335 genomic variations between KitaakeX and Nipponbare (ssp. japonica), and 2,785,991 variations between KitaakeX and Zhenshan97 (ssp. indica). We also compared Kitaake resequencing reads to the KitaakeX assembly and identified 219 small variations. The high-quality genome of the model rice plant KitaakeX will accelerate rice functional genomics. The high quality, de novo assembly of the KitaakeX genome will serve as a useful reference genome for rice and will accelerate functional genomics studies of rice and other species."/>

    <meta name="description" content="The availability of thousands of complete rice genome sequences from diverse varieties and accessions has laid the foundation for in-depth exploration of the rice genome. One drawback to these collections is that most of these rice varieties have long life cycles, and/or low transformation efficiencies, which limits their usefulness as model organisms for functional genomics studies. In contrast, the rice variety Kitaake has a rapid life cycle (9&#8201;weeks seed to seed) and is easy to transform and propagate. For these reasons, Kitaake has emerged as a model for studies of diverse monocotyledonous species. Here, we report the de novo genome sequencing and analysis of Oryza sativa ssp. japonica variety KitaakeX, a Kitaake plant carrying the rice XA21 immune receptor. Our KitaakeX sequence assembly contains 377.6&#8201;Mb, consisting of 33 scaffolds (476 contigs) with a contig N50 of 1.4&#8201;Mb. Complementing the assembly are detailed gene annotations of 35,594 protein coding genes. We identified 331,335 genomic variations between KitaakeX and Nipponbare (ssp. japonica), and 2,785,991 variations between KitaakeX and Zhenshan97 (ssp. indica). We also compared Kitaake resequencing reads to the KitaakeX assembly and identified 219 small variations. The high-quality genome of the model rice plant KitaakeX will accelerate rice functional genomics. The high quality, de novo assembly of the KitaakeX genome will serve as a useful reference genome for rice and will accelerate functional genomics studies of rice and other species."/>

    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();

    <script>
        function OptanonWrapper() {
            var elementInside = function(candidate, element) {
                if (candidate === element) {
                    return true;
                } else if (candidate.nodeName.toLowerCase() === 'body') {
                    return false;
                } else {
                    return elementInside(candidate.parentNode, element);
                }
            };

            var disclaimer = document.querySelector('.c-disclaimer[aria-hidden="false"]');
            window.dataLayer.push({event:'OneTrustGroupsUpdated'});
            if (disclaimer) {
                if (!elementInside(document.activeElement, disclaimer)) {
                    disclaimer.querySelector('button').focus();
                }
            } else {
                document.activeElement.blur();
            }
        }

        <meta property="og:site_name" content="BMC Genomics"/>
        <meta property="og:title" content="Genome sequence of the model rice variety KitaakeX"/>
        <meta property="og:description" content="The availability of thousands of complete rice genome sequences from diverse varieties and accessions has laid the foundation for in-depth exploration of the rice genome. One drawback to these collections is that most of these rice varieties have long life cycles, and/or low transformation efficiencies, which limits their usefulness as model organisms for functional genomics studies. In contrast, the rice variety Kitaake has a rapid life cycle (9weeks seed to seed) and is easy to transform and propagate. For these reasons, Kitaake has emerged as a model for studies of diverse monocotyledonous species. Here, we report the de novo genome sequencing and analysis of Oryza sativa ssp. japonica variety KitaakeX, a Kitaake plant carrying the rice XA21 immune receptor. Our KitaakeX sequence assembly contains 377.6Mb, consisting of 33 scaffolds (476 contigs) with a contig N50 of 1.4Mb. Complementing the assembly are detailed gene annotations of 35,594 protein coding genes. We identified 331,335 genomic variations between KitaakeX and Nipponbare (ssp. japonica), and 2,785,991 variations between KitaakeX and Zhenshan97 (ssp. indica). We also compared Kitaake resequencing reads to the KitaakeX assembly and identified 219 small variations. The high-quality genome of the model rice plant KitaakeX will accelerate rice functional genomics. The high quality, de novo assembly of the KitaakeX genome will serve as a useful reference genome for rice and will accelerate functional genomics studies of rice and other species."/>
        <meta property="og:image" content="https://media.springernature.com/w200/springer-static/cover/journal/12864.jpg"/>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Background</h3><p>The availability of thousands of complete rice genome sequences from diverse varieties and accessions has laid the foundation for in-depth exploration of the rice genome. One drawback to these collections is that most of these rice varieties have long life cycles, and/or low transformation efficiencies, which limits their usefulness as model organisms for functional genomics studies. In contrast, the rice variety Kitaake has a rapid life cycle (9weeks seed to seed) and is easy to transform and propagate. For these reasons, Kitaake has emerged as a model for studies of diverse monocotyledonous species.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Results</h3><p>Here, we report the de novo genome sequencing and analysis of <i>Oryza sativa ssp. japonica</i> variety KitaakeX, a Kitaake plant carrying the rice XA21 immune receptor. Our KitaakeX sequence assembly contains 377.6Mb, consisting of 33 scaffolds (476 contigs) with a contig N50 of 1.4Mb. Complementing the assembly are detailed gene annotations of 35,594 protein coding genes. We identified 331,335 genomic variations between KitaakeX and Nipponbare (ssp. <i>japonica</i>), and 2,785,991 variations between KitaakeX and Zhenshan97 (ssp. <i>indica</i>). We also compared Kitaake resequencing reads to the KitaakeX assembly and identified 219 small variations. The high-quality genome of the model rice plant KitaakeX will accelerate rice functional genomics.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Conclusions</h3><p>The high quality, de novo assembly of the KitaakeX genome will serve as a useful reference genome for rice and will accelerate functional genomics studies of rice and other species.</p></div></div></section>
                    
    


                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Background"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Background</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Rice (<i>Oryza sativa</i>) provides food for more than half of the worlds population [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Gross BL, Zhao Z. Archaeological and genetic insights into the origins of domesticated rice. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2014;111(17):61907." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1130">1</a>] and also serves as a model for studies of other monocotyledonous species. Cultivated rice contains two major types of <i>O. sativa</i>, the <i>O. sativa indica/Xian</i> group and the <i>O. sativa japonica/Geng</i> group. Using genomic markers, two additional minor types have been recognized, the circum-Aus group and the circum-Basmati group [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Wang W, Mauleon R, Hu Z, Chebotarov D, Tai S, Wu Z, Li M, Zheng T, Fuentes RR, Zhang FJN. Genomic variation in 3,010 diverse accessions of Asian cultivated rice. Nature. 2018;557(7703):43." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1143">2</a>]. More than 3000 rice varieties and species have been sequenced, including Nipponbare [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Kawahara Y, de la Bastide M, Hamilton JP, Kanamori H, McCombie WR, Ouyang S, Schwartz DC, Tanaka T, Wu J, Zhou S, et al. Improvement of the Oryza sativa Nipponbare reference genome using next generation sequence and optical map data. Rice (N Y). 2013;6(1):4." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1146">3</a>], 9311 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Yu J, Hu S, Wang J, Wong GK-S, Li S, Liu B, Deng Y, Dai L, Zhou Y, Zhang XJ. A draft sequence of the rice genome (Oryza sativa L. ssp. indica). Science. 2002;296(5565):7992." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1149">4</a>], DJ 123, IR64 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Schatz MC, Maron LG, Stein JC, Wences AH, Gurtowski J, Biggers E, Lee H, Kramer M, Antoniou E, Ghiban EJG. Whole genome de novo assemblies of three divergent strains of rice, Oryza sativa, document novel gene space of aus and indica. Genome Biol. 2014;15(11):506." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1152">5</a>], Zhenshan97, Minghui 63 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Zhang J, Chen L-L, Xing F, Kudrna DA, Yao W, Copetti D, Mu T, Li W, Song J-M, Xie W et al: Extensive sequence divergence between the reference genomes of two elite &lt;em&gt;indica&lt;/em&gt; rice varieties Zhenshan 97 and Minghui 63. 2016, 113(35):E5163-E5171." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1155">6</a>], Shuhui498 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Du H, Yu Y, Ma Y, Gao Q, Cao Y, Chen Z, Ma B, Qi M, Li Y, Zhao XJN. Sequencing and de novo assembly of a near complete indica rice genome. Nat Commun. 2017;8:15324." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1158">7</a>], <i>Oryza glaberrima</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Wang M, Yu Y, Haberer G, Marri PR, Fan C, Goicoechea JL, Zuccolo A, Song X, Kudrna D, Ammiraju JS, et al. The genome sequence of African rice (Oryza glaberrima) and evidence for independent domestication. Nat Genet. 2014;46(9):9828." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1165">8</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Wang W, Mauleon R, Hu Z, Chebotarov D, Tai S, Wu Z, Li M, Zheng T, Fuentes RR, Zhang FJN. Genomic variation in 3,010 diverse accessions of Asian cultivated rice. Nature. 2018;557(7703):43." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1168">2</a>]. The availability of these genomes has laid a strong foundation for basic rice research and breeding [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Wang W, Mauleon R, Hu Z, Chebotarov D, Tai S, Wu Z, Li M, Zheng T, Fuentes RR, Zhang FJN. Genomic variation in 3,010 diverse accessions of Asian cultivated rice. Nature. 2018;557(7703):43." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1171">2</a>]. However, the use of these sequenced varieties for functional genomics analyses is limited by their long life cycles or low transformation efficiencies. For example, it takes up to 6months for Nipponbare to produce seeds under winter conditions. The Indica varieties typically have relatively low transformation efficiencies [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Kim SL, Choi M, Jung KH, An G. Analysis of the early-flowering mechanisms and generation of T-DNA tagging lines in Kitaake, a model rice cultivar. J Exp Bot. 2013;64(14):416982." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1174">9</a>].</p><p>The Kitaake cultivar (ssp. <i>japonica</i>), which originated at the northern limit of rice cultivation in Hokkaido, Japan [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="lchitani K, Okumoto Y, Tanisaka T. Photoperiod sensitivity gene of se-1 locus found in photoperiod insensitive rice cultivars of the northern limit region of rice cultivation. Breed Sci. 1997;47:8." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1183">10</a>], has emerged as a model for rice research [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Kim SL, Choi M, Jung KH, An G. Analysis of the early-flowering mechanisms and generation of T-DNA tagging lines in Kitaake, a model rice cultivar. J Exp Bot. 2013;64(14):416982." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1186">9</a>]. Kitaake is insensitive to day length, easy to propagate, relatively cold tolerant, short in stature and completes its life cycle in about 9weeks [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Kim SL, Choi M, Jung KH, An G. Analysis of the early-flowering mechanisms and generation of T-DNA tagging lines in Kitaake, a model rice cultivar. J Exp Bot. 2013;64(14):416982." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1189">9</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Kunihiro Y, Ebe Y, Wada S, Shinbashi N, Honma A, Sasaki T, Sasaki K, Numao Y, Morimura K, Tan No H. The new rice variety Kita-ake. Bulletin of Hokkaido prefectural agricultural experiment stations. 1989;59:4." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1192">11</a>]. These properties make it easy to cultivate under typical greenhouse conditions. Kitaake is also highly amenable to transformation [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Jung KH, An G, Ronald PC. Towards a better bowl of rice: assigning function to tens of thousands of rice genes. Nat Rev Genet. 2008;9(2):91101." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1196">12</a>]. Several hundred genes have been overexpressed or silenced in KitaakeX [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Jung KH, An G, Ronald PC. Towards a better bowl of rice: assigning function to tens of thousands of rice genes. Nat Rev Genet. 2008;9(2):91101." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1199">12</a>]. The transformation efficiency of Kitaake is comparable to that of that Dongjin, a cultivar that historically transforms well [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Kim SL, Choi M, Jung KH, An G. Analysis of the early-flowering mechanisms and generation of T-DNA tagging lines in Kitaake, a model rice cultivar. J Exp Bot. 2013;64(14):416982." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1202">9</a>]. Kitaake has been used to establish multiple mutant populations, including an RNAi mutant collection [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Wang L, Zheng J, Luo Y, Xu T, Zhang Q, Zhang L, Xu M, Wan J, Wang MB, Zhang CJPJ. Construction of a genomewide RNA i mutant library in rice. Plant Biotechnol J. 2013;11(8):9971005." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1205">13</a>], T-DNA insertion collections [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Kim SL, Choi M, Jung KH, An G. Analysis of the early-flowering mechanisms and generation of T-DNA tagging lines in Kitaake, a model rice cultivar. J Exp Bot. 2013;64(14):416982." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1208">9</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Gao H, Zheng XM, Fei G, Chen J, Jin M, Ren Y, Wu W, Zhou K, Sheng P, Zhou F, et al. Ehd4 encodes a novel and Oryza-genus-specific regulator of photoperiodic flowering in rice. PLoS Genet. 2013;9(2):e1003281." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1211">14</a>], and a whole-genome sequenced mutant population of KitaakeX, a Kitaake variety carrying the <i>Xa21</i> immune receptor gene (formerly called X.Kitaake) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Li G, Jain R, Chern M, Pham NT, Martin JA, Wei T, Schackwitz WS, Lipzen AM, Duong PQ, Jones KC, et al. The sequences of 1504 mutants in the model Rice variety Kitaake facilitate rapid functional genomic studies. Plant Cell. 2017;29(6):121831." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1218">15</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Song WY, Wang GL, Chen LL, Kim HS, Pi LY, Holsten T, Gardner J, Wang B, Zhai WX, Zhu LH, et al. A receptor kinase-like protein encoded by the rice disease resistance gene, Xa21. Science. 1995;270(5243):18046." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1221">16</a>]. Kitaake has been used to explore diverse aspects of rice biology, including flowering time [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Gao H, Jin M, Zheng X-M, Chen J, Yuan D, Xin Y, Wang M, Huang D, Zhang Z, Zhou KJPNAS. Days to heading 7, a major quantitative locus determining photoperiod sensitivity and regional adaptation in rice. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A, 16342. 2014;111(46):16337." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1224">17</a>], disease resistance [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ronald PC, Beutler B. Plant and animal sensors of conserved microbial signatures. Science. 2010;330(6007):10614." href="#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1227">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Liu Y, Wu H, Chen H, Liu Y, He J, Kang H, Sun Z, Pan G, Wang Q, Hu JJN. A gene cluster encoding lectin receptor kinases confers broad-spectrum and durable insect resistance in rice. Nat Biotechnol. 2015;33(3):301." href="#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1227_1">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Zhou X, Liao H, Chern M, Yin J, Chen Y, Wang J, Zhu X, Chen Z, Yuan C, Zhao W, et al. Loss of function of a rice TPR-domain RNA-binding protein confers broad-spectrum disease resistance. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2018;115(12):31749." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1230">20</a>], small RNA biology [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Rodrigues JA, Ruan R, Nishimura T, Sharma MK, Sharma R, Ronald PC, Fischer RL, Zilberman D. Imprinted expression of genes and small RNA is associated with localized hypomethylation of the maternal genome in rice endosperm. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2013;110(19):79349." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1234">21</a>], and the CRISPR-Cas9 and TALEN technologies [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Li T, Liu B, Spalding MH, Weeks DP, Yang B. High-efficiency TALEN-based gene editing produces disease-resistant rice. Nat Biotechnol. 2012;30(5):3902." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1237">22</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Xie K, Minkenberg B, Yang Y. Boosting CRISPR/Cas9 multiplex editing capability with the endogenous tRNA-processing system. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2015;112(11):35705." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1240">23</a>].</p><p>The unavailability of the Kitaake genome sequence has posed an obstacle to the use of Kitaake in rice research. For example, analysis of a fast-neutron (FN) induced mutant population in KitaakeX, a Kitaake plant carrying the rice XA21 gene [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Li G, Jain R, Chern M, Pham NT, Martin JA, Wei T, Schackwitz WS, Lipzen AM, Duong PQ, Jones KC, et al. The sequences of 1504 mutants in the model Rice variety Kitaake facilitate rapid functional genomic studies. Plant Cell. 2017;29(6):121831." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1246">15</a>], required the use of Nipponbare (ssp. <i>japonica)</i> as the reference genome. Additionally, CRISPR/Cas9 guide RNAs cannot be accurately designed for Kitaake without a complete sequence. To address these issues, we assembled a high-quality genome sequence of KitaakeX, compared its genome to the genomes of rice varieties Nipponbare and Zhenshan97 (ssp. <i>indica</i>), and identified genomic variations. The XA21 gene confers resistance to the bacterial pathogen, <i>Xanthomonas oryzae pv. oryzae,</i> making KItaakeX a model for studies of infectious disease [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Song WY, Wang GL, Chen LL, Kim HS, Pi LY, Holsten T, Gardner J, Wang B, Zhai WX, Zhu LH, et al. A receptor kinase-like protein encoded by the rice disease resistance gene, Xa21. Science. 1995;270(5243):18046." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1258">16</a>].</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">KitaakeX flowers significantly earlier than other sequenced rice varieties</h3><p>Kitaake has long been recognized as a rapid life-cycle variety [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Jung KH, An G, Ronald PC. Towards a better bowl of rice: assigning function to tens of thousands of rice genes. Nat Rev Genet. 2008;9(2):91101." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1273">12</a>], but it has yet to be systematically compared to other rice varieties. We compared the flowering time of KitaakeX with other sequenced rice varieties under long-day conditions (14h light/10h dark). Consistent with other studies, we found that KitaakeX flowers much earlier than other varieties (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#Fig1">1</a>a, b), heading at 54days after germination. Other rice varieties Nipponbare, 9311 (ssp. <i>indica</i>), IR64 (ssp. <i>indica</i>), Zhenshan 97, Minghui 63 (ssp. <i>indica</i>), and Kasalath (aus rice cultivar) start heading at 134, 99, 107, 79, 125, and 84days after germination, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#Fig1">1</a>b).
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-019-6262-4/MediaObjects/12864_2019_6262_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-019-6262-4/MediaObjects/12864_2019_6262_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="627" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>The early flowering rice variety KitaakeX; <b>a</b> KitaakeX and selected sequenced rice varieties under long-day conditions. Scale bar=10cm; <b>b</b> Flowering time of KitaakeX and selected rice varieties under long-day conditions. DAG, days after germination. Asterisks indicate significant differences using the unpaired Students <i>t</i>-test (<i>P</i>&lt;0.0001); We used 18 KitaakeX, 30 Nipponbare, 16 9311, 21 IR64, 20 Zhenshan97, 19 Minghui 63, and 15 Kasalath plants to measure heading date. <b>c</b> KitaakeX in the unweighted neighbor-joining tree comprising 3010 accessions of the 3k rice genomes project and indicated varieties. It includes four XI clusters (XI-1A from East Asia, XI-1B of modern varieties of diverse origins, XI-2 from South Asia and XI-3 from Southeast Asia); three GJ clusters [primarily East Asian temperate (named GJ-tmp), Southeast Asian subtropical (named GJ-sbtrp) and Southeast Asian Tropical (named GJ-trp)]; and two groups for the mostly South Asian cA (circum-Aus) and cB (circum-Basmati) accessions, 1 group Admix (accessions that fall between major groups were classified as admixed) Branch length indicates the genetic distance between two haplotypes</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We next assessed how KitaakeX is related to other rice varieties using a phylogenetic approach based on the rice population structure and diversity published for 3010 varieties [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Wang W, Mauleon R, Hu Z, Chebotarov D, Tai S, Wu Z, Li M, Zheng T, Fuentes RR, Zhang FJN. Genomic variation in 3,010 diverse accessions of Asian cultivated rice. Nature. 2018;557(7703):43." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1318">2</a>]. The 3010 sequenced accessions were classified into nine subpopulations, most of which could be connected to geographical origins. The phylogenetic tree reveals that KitaakeX and Nipponbare are closely related within the same subpopulation (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#Fig1">1</a>c).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>To obtain a high-quality, de novo genome assembly, we sequenced the KitaakeX genome using a strategy that combines short-read and long-read sequencing. Sequencing reads were collected using Illumina, 10x Genomics, PACBIO, and Sanger platforms at the Joint Genome Institute (JGI) and the HudsonAlpha Institute. The current release is version 3.0, which is a combination of a MECAT (Mapping, Error Correction and de novo Assembly Tools) PACBIO based assembly and an Illumina sequenced 10x genomics SuperNova assembly. The assembled sequence contains 377.6Mb, consisting of 33 scaffolds (476 contigs) with a contig N50 of 1.4Mb, covering a total of 99.67% of assembled bases in chromosomes (Table<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#Tab1">1</a>.a).
</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Summary of the KitaakeX genome assembly and annotation</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We assessed the quality of the KitaakeX assembly for sequence completeness and accuracy. Completeness of the assembly was assessed by aligning the 34,651 annotated genes from the v7.0 Nipponbare to the KitaakeX assembly using BLAT [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Kent WJ. BLAT--the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 2002;12(4):65664." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1460">24</a>]. The alignments indicate that 98.94% (34,285 of genes) genes completely aligned to the KitaakeX assembly, 0.75% (259 genes) partially aligned, and 0.31% (107 genes) were not detected. A bacterial artificial chromosome (BAC) library was constructed and a set of 346 BAC clones (9.2x clone coverage) was sequenced using PACBIO sequencing. A range of variants was detected by comparing the BAC clones to the assembly. Alignments were of high quality (&lt;0.1% of error) in 271 clones (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S13). Sixty BACs indicate a higher error rate (0.45% of error) due mainly to their placement in repetitive regions (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S14). Fifteen BAC clones indicate a rearrangement (10 clones) or a putative overlap on adjacent contigs (5 clones) (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S15). The overall error rate in the BAC clones is 0.09%, indicating the high quality of this assembly (for detailed information, see Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#MOESM1">1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genome annotation</h3><p>We predicted 35,594 protein-coding genes in the KitaakeX genome (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#Tab1">1</a>.c, Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S12), representing 31.5% genic space of the assembled genome size (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#Tab1">1</a>). There is some transcriptome support for 89.5% (31,854/35,594) of the KitaakeX genes, and 81.6% (29,039/35,594) genes are fully supported by the transcriptome (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S11). The predicted protein-coding genes are distributed unevenly across each chromosome; gene density tends to be higher toward chromosome ends (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#Fig2">2</a>f). The average GC content of the genome is 43.7% (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#Fig2">2</a>e, Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#Tab1">1</a>.a).
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-019-6262-4/MediaObjects/12864_2019_6262_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-019-6262-4/MediaObjects/12864_2019_6262_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="673" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Genome wide analysis of KitaakeX genome and its comparison with other rice varieties; <b>a</b> Circles indicate the 12 KitaakeX chromosomes represented on a Mb scale; <b>b</b>, <b>c</b> SNPs and InDels between KitaakeX and Nipponbare (<b>b</b>) and KitaakeX and Zhenshan97 (<b>c</b>); <b>d</b> Repeat density; <b>e</b> GC content; <b>f</b> Gene density; <b>g</b> Homologous genes in the KitaakeX genome. Window size used in the circles is 500kb</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To assess the quality of the annotation of KitaakeX genes, we compared the KitaakeX annotation to those of other completed rice genomes using the BUSCO v2 method, which is based on a set of 1440 conserved plant genes. The results confirm 99.0% completeness of the KitaakeX genome annotation (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#Tab1">1</a>, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S7). To further evaluate the quality of the annotation, we studied the extent of conservation of functional genes in KitaakeX. We selected 291 genes (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#MOESM3">3</a>: Table S13) from three pathways associated with stress resistance, flowering time and response to light [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Wang M, Yu Y, Haberer G, Marri PR, Fan C, Goicoechea JL, Zuccolo A, Song X, Kudrna D, Ammiraju JS, et al. The genome sequence of African rice (Oryza glaberrima) and evidence for independent domestication. Nat Genet. 2014;46(9):9828." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1553">8</a>], and then searched for orthologous genes in the KitaakeX genome. We found that 275 of 291 (94.5%) of the selected KitaakeX genes show greater than 90% identity with the corresponding Nipponbare genes at the protein level. Twenty-three out of the 291 show 100% identity at the nucleotide level but not at the protein level. Of these 23 genes, the KitaakeX gene model for 16 genes has better transcriptomic evidence than the Nipponbare gene model. One of the 291 KitaakeX genes is slightly shorter than its Nipponbare ortholog due to an alternative transcript (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#MOESM3">3</a>: Table S13). These results indicate the high quality of the annotation, and conservation between the KitaakeX and Nipponbare <i>japonica</i> rice varieties.</p><p>Using SynMap, we identified 2469 pairs of colinear genes (88 blocks) in the KitaakeX genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#Fig2">2</a>g). These results correlate with already published findings [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Guyot R, Keller BJG: Ancestral genome duplication in rice. 2004, 47(3):610614." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1569">25</a>]. We used RepeatMaker and Blaster to identify transposable elements (TEs) in the KitaakeX genome, and identified 122.2Mb of sequence corresponding to TEs (32.0% of the genome). DNA transposons account for ~33Mb; retrotransposons account for ~90Mb. The TEs belong mostly to the Gypsy and Copia retroelement families, and account for 23% of the genome (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S8), as is true in the Nipponbare and Zhenshan97 genomes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Zhang J, Chen L-L, Xing F, Kudrna DA, Yao W, Copetti D, Mu T, Li W, Song J-M, Xie W et al: Extensive sequence divergence between the reference genomes of two elite &lt;em&gt;indica&lt;/em&gt; rice varieties Zhenshan 97 and Minghui 63. 2016, 113(35):E5163-E5171." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1575">6</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Genomic variations between KitaakeX and other rice varieties</h3><p>We compared the genome of KitaakeX to the Nipponbare and Zhenshan97 genomes to detect genomic variations, including single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs), insertions and deletions under 30bp (InDels), presence/absence variations (PAVs), and inversions using MUMmer [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Kurtz S, Phillippy A, Delcher AL, Smoot M, Shumway M, Antonescu C, Salzberg SLJG. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biol. 2004;5(2):R12." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1586">26</a>]. We found 331,335 variations between KitaakeX and Nipponbare (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#MOESM4">4</a>), and nearly 10 times as many (2,785,991) variations between KitaakeX and Zhenshan97 (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#MOESM5">5</a>). There are 253,295 SNPs and 75,183 InDels between KitaakeX and Nipponbare, and 2,328,319 SNPs and 442,962 InDels between KitaakeX and Zhenshan97 (Additionalfiles<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#MOESM6">6</a> and Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S3). With respect to SNPs in both intersubspecies (<i>japonica</i> vs. <i>indica</i>) as well as intrasubspecies (<i>japonica</i> vs. <i>japonica</i>) comparisons, transitions (Tss) (G -&gt;A and C -&gt;T) are about twice as abundant as transversions (Tvs) (G -&gt;C and C -&gt;G) (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S10). Genomic variations between KitaakeX and Nipponbare are highly concentrated in some genomic regions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#Fig2">2</a>b), but variations between KitaakeX and Zhenshan97 are spread evenly through the genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#Fig2">2</a>c). Intersubspecies genomic variations, then, are much more extensive than intrasubspecies variations. We also detected multiple genomic inversions using comparative genomics (Additionalfiles<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#MOESM4">4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#MOESM5">5</a>).</p><p>For variations occurring in the genic regions, we found that single-base and 3bp (without frame shift) InDels are much more abundant than others (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#MOESM7">7</a>: Figure S16a), suggesting that these genetic variations have been functionally selected. We carried out detailed analysis of gene structure alterations that exist as a consequence of SNPs and InDels between KitaakeX and Nipponbare and Kitaake and Zhenshan97. Between KitaakeX and Nipponbare, we identified 2092 frameshifts, 78 changes affecting splice-site acceptors, 71 changes affecting splice-site donors, 19 lost start codons, 161 gained stop codons, and 15 lost stop codons. In the comparison of KitaakeX to Zhenshan97, 6809 unique genes in KitaakeX are affected by 8640 frameshifts (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#MOESM7">7</a>: Figure S16b), 531 changes affecting splice-site acceptors, 530 changes affecting splice-site donors, 185 lost start codons, 902 gained stop codons and 269 lost stop codons (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#MOESM7">7</a>: Figure S16b).</p><p>Based on PAV analysis, we identified 456 loci that are specific to KitaakeX (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#MOESM4">4</a>) compared with Nipponbare. Pfam analysis of KitaakeX-specific regions revealed 275 proteins. Out of these 275 genes, 148 genes are from 19 different gene families with more than 2 genes in those regions. These gene families include protein kinases, leucine-rich repeat proteins, NB-ARC domain-containing proteins, F-box domain containing proteins, protein tyrosine kinases, Myb/SANt-like DNA binding domain proteins, transferase family proteins, xylanase inhibitor C-terminal protein, and plant proteins of unknown function (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#MOESM7">7</a>: Figure S16c). We identified 4589 loci specific to KitaakeX compared with Zhenshan97 (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#MOESM5">5</a>).</p><p>We also compared our de novo assembly of KitaakeX genome with Kitaake resequencing reads using an established pipeline [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Li G, Jain R, Chern M, Pham NT, Martin JA, Wei T, Schackwitz WS, Lipzen AM, Duong PQ, Jones KC, et al. The sequences of 1504 mutants in the model Rice variety Kitaake facilitate rapid functional genomic studies. Plant Cell. 2017;29(6):121831." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1657">15</a>]. This analysis revealed 219 small variations (200 SNPs and 19 INDELs) between the two genomes (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#MOESM8">8</a>). These variations affect 9 genes in KitaakeX besides the Ubi-<i>Xa21</i> transgene, including the selectable marker encoding a hygromycin B phosphotransferase on chromosome 6 (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#MOESM8">8</a>, Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#MOESM9">9</a>: Figure S17).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><p>In 2005 the Nipponbare genome was sequenced and annotated to a high-quality level (International Rice Genome Sequencing and Sasaki 2005). Since that time, it has served as a reference genome for many rice genomic studies [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Matsumoto T, Wu J, Itoh T, Numa H, Antonio B, Sasaki T. The Nipponbare genome and the next-generation of rice genomics research in Japan. Rice (New York, NY). 2016;9(1):33." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1681">27</a>]. Despite its use, the long life cycle of Nipponbare makes it time-consuming for most genetic analyses.</p><p>Here we report the de novo assembly and annotation of KitaakeX, an early-flowering rice variety with a rapid life cycle that is easy to propagate under greenhouse conditions. We predict that KitaakeX contains 35,594 protein-coding genes, comparable to the published genomes (39,045 for Nipponbare and 34,610 for Zhenshan97) (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#MOESM4">4</a> and Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#MOESM5">5</a>). The availability of a high-quality genome and annotation for KitaakeX will be useful for associating traits of interest with genetic variations, and for identifying the genes controlling those traits.</p><p>We identified 219 SNPs and InDels between the KitaakeX and Kitaake genomes. These variations may have resulted from somatic mutations that arose during tissue culture and regeneration, or they may be spontaneous mutations [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Tang X, Liu G, Zhou J, Ren Q, You Q, Tian L, Xin X, Zhong Z, Liu B, Zheng XJG. A large-scale whole-genome sequencing analysis reveals highly specific genome editing by both Cas9 and Cpf1 (Cas12a) nucleases in rice. Genome Biol. 2018;19(1):84." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1696">28</a>]. For rice, 150 mutations are typically induced during tissue culture and 41 mutations occur spontaneously per three generations [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Tang X, Liu G, Zhou J, Ren Q, You Q, Tian L, Xin X, Zhong Z, Liu B, Zheng XJG. A large-scale whole-genome sequencing analysis reveals highly specific genome editing by both Cas9 and Cpf1 (Cas12a) nucleases in rice. Genome Biol. 2018;19(1):84." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1699">28</a>]. These numbers are consistent with the independent propagation of KitaakeX and Kitaake over approximately 10 generations in the greenhouse.</p><p>The KitaakeX genome will be useful for variety of studies. For example, we recently published the whole genome sequences of 1504 FN-mutated KitaakeX rice lines. Mutations were identified by aligning reads of the KitaakeX mutants to the Nipponbare reference genome [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Li G, Jain R, Chern M, Pham NT, Martin JA, Wei T, Schackwitz WS, Lipzen AM, Duong PQ, Jones KC, et al. The sequences of 1504 mutants in the model Rice variety Kitaake facilitate rapid functional genomic studies. Plant Cell. 2017;29(6):121831." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1705">15</a>]. On average, 97% of the Nipponbare genome is covered by the KitaakeX reads. However, in some regions, the KitaakeX genome diverges from Nipponbare to such an extent that no variants can be confidently identified. These appear either as gaps in coverage or as regions containing a concentration of natural variations between KitaakeX and Nipponbare. We can now use the KitaakeX sequence as the direct reference genome and detect mutations in highly variable regions. This approach will simplify analysis and increase confidence in the identification of FN-induced mutations. Because there are only 219 small variations between KitaakeX and Kitaake (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#MOESM8">8</a>), the KitaakeX genome can also be used as the reference genome for Kitaake.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Conclusions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Conclusions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>The de novo assembly of the KitaakeX genome serves as a useful reference genome for the model rice variety Kitaake and will facilitate investigations into the genetic basis of diverse traits critical for rice biology and genetic improvement.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Plant materials and growth conditions</h3><p>Dr. Thomas W. Okita from Washington State University provided the Kitaake seeds, which were originally obtained from Dr. Hiroyuki Ito, Akita National College of Technology, Japan. Dr. Jan E. Leach at Colorado State University provided seeds for Zhenshan 97, Minghui 63, IR64 and 9311. Seeds of Kasalath were provided by the USDA Dale Bumpers National Rice Research Center, Stuttgart, Arkansas. Seeds were germinated on 1/2x MS (Murashige and Skoog) medium. Seedlings were transferred to a greenhouse and planted 3 plants/pot during the springtime (Mar. 2, 2017) in Davis, California. The light intensity was set at approximately 250molm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>1</sup>. The day/night period was set to 14/10h, and the temperature was set between 28 and 30C [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Schwessinger B, Bahar O, Thomas N, Holton N, Nekrasov V, Ruan D, Canlas PE, Daudi A, Petzold CJ, Singan VR, et al. Transgenic expression of the dicotyledonous pattern recognition receptor EFR in rice leads to ligand-dependent activation of defense responses. PLoS Pathog. 2015;11(3):e1004809." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1735">29</a>]. Rice plants were grown in sandy soil supplemented with nutrient water. The day when the first panicle of the plant emerged was recorded as the heading date for that plant. Kasalath seeds were received later, and the heading date was recorded in the same way. The experiment was repeated in winter.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Construction of a phylogenetic tree</h3><p>We obtained 178,496 evenly distributed SNPs by dividing the genome into 3.8kb bins and selecting one or two SNPs per bin randomly according to the SNP density of the bin. Genotypes of all the rice accessions, including 3010 accessions of the 3K Rice Genomes Project and additional noted accessions, were fetched from the SNP database RiceVarMap v2.0 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Zhao H, Yao W, Ouyang Y, Yang W, Wang G, Lian X, Xing Y, Chen L, Xie W. RiceVarMap: a comprehensive database of rice genomic variations. Nucleic Acids Res. 2015;43(Database issue):D101822." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1746">30</a>] and related genomic data [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Li G, Chern M, Jain R, Martin JA, Schackwitz WS, Jiang L, Vega-Sanchez ME, Lipzen AM, Barry KW, Schmutz J, et al. Genome-wide sequencing of 41 Rice (Oryza sativa L.) mutated lines reveals diverse mutations induced by fast-neutron irradiation. Mol Plant. 2016;9(7):107881." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1749">31</a>] and used to calculate an IBS distance matrix which was then applied to construct a phylogenetic tree by the unweighted neighbor-joining method, implemented in the R package APE [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Paradis E, Claude J, Strimmer K. APE: analyses of Phylogenetics and evolution in R language. Bioinformatics. 2004;20(2):28990." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1752">32</a>]. Branches of the phylogenetic tree were colored according to the classification of the 3010 rice accessions [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Wang W, Mauleon R, Hu Z, Chebotarov D, Tai S, Wu Z, Li M, Zheng T, Fuentes RR, Zhang FJN. Genomic variation in 3,010 diverse accessions of Asian cultivated rice. Nature. 2018;557(7703):43." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1755">2</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>High molecular weight DNA from young leaves of KitaakeX was isolated and used in sequencing. See (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#MOESM1">1</a>) for further details.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Annotation of protein-coding genes</h3><p>To obtain high-quality annotations, we performed high throughput RNA-seq analysis of libraries from diverse rice tissues (leaf, stem, panicle, and root). Approximately 683 million pairs of 2151 paired-end RNA-seq reads were obtained and assembled using a comprehensive pipeline PERTRAN (unpublished). Gene models were predicted by combining ab initio gene prediction, protein-based homology searches, experimentally cloned cDNAs/expressed-sequence tags (ESTs) and assembled transcripts from the RNA-seq data. Gene functions were further annotated according to the best-matched proteins from the SwissProt and TrEMBL databases [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Bairoch A, Apweiler R. The SWISS-PROT protein sequence database and its supplement TrEMBL in 2000. Nucleic Acids Res. 2000;28(1):458." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1777">33</a>] using BLASTP (E value &lt;10<sup>5</sup>) (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#MOESM11">11</a>). Genes without hits in these databases were annotated as hypothetical proteins. Gene Ontology (GO) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Dutkowski J, Kramer M, Surma MA, Balakrishnan R, Cherry JM, Krogan NJ, Ideker T. A gene ontology inferred from molecular networks. Nat Biotechnol. 2013;31(1):3845." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1785">34</a>] term assignments and protein domains and motifs were extracted with InterPro [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Finn RD, Attwood TK, Babbitt PC, Bateman A, Bork P, Bridge AJ, Chang HY, Dosztanyi Z, El-Gebali S, Fraser M, et al. InterPro in 2017-beyond protein family and domain annotations. Nucleic Acids Res. 2017;45(D1):D1909." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1788">35</a>]. Pathway analysis was derived from the best-match eukaryotic protein in the Kyoto encyclopedia of genes and genomes (KEGG) database [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Kanehisa M, Furumichi M, Tanabe M, Sato Y, Morishima K. KEGG: new perspectives on genomes, pathways, diseases and drugs. Nucleic Acids Res. 2017;45(D1):D35361." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1792">36</a>] using BLASTP (E value&lt;1.0e<sup>10</sup>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Genome Synteny</h3><p>We used SynMap (CoGe, <a href="http://www.genomevolution.org">www.genomevolution.org</a>) to identify collinearity blocks using homologous CDS pairs with parameters according to Daccord et al. [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Daccord N, Celton JM, Linsmith G, Becker C, Choisne N, Schijlen E, van de Geest H, Bianco L, Micheletti D, Velasco R, et al. High-quality de novo assembly of the apple genome and methylome dynamics of early fruit development. Nat Genet. 2017;49(7):1099106." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1810">37</a>] and visualized collinearity blocks using Circos [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Krzywinski M, Schein J, Birol I, Connors J, Gascoyne R, Horsman D, Jones SJ, Marra MA. Circos: an information aesthetic for comparative genomics. Genome Res. 2009;19(9):163945." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1813">38</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Repeat annotation</h3><p>The fraction of transposable elements and repeated sequences in the assembly was obtained merging the output of RepeatMasker (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>, v. 3.3.0) and Blaster (a component of the REPET package) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Flutre T, Duprat E, Feuillet C, Quesneville H. Considering transposable element diversification in de novo annotation approaches. PLoS One. 2011;6(1):e16526." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1828">39</a>]. The two programs were run using nucleotide libraries (PReDa and RepeatExplorer) from RiTE-db [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Copetti D, Zhang J, El Baidouri M, Gao D, Wang J, Barghini E, Cossu RM, Angelova A, Maldonado LC, Roffler S, et al. RiTE database: a resource database for genus-wide rice genomics and evolutionary biology. BMC Genomics. 2015;16:538." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1831">40</a>] and an in-house curated collection of transposable element (TE) proteins, respectively. Reconciliation of masked repeats was carried out using custom Perl scripts and formatted in gff3 files. Infernal [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Nawrocki EP, Eddy SR. Infernal 1.1: 100-fold faster RNA homology searches. Bioinformatics. 2013;29(22):29335." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1834">41</a>] was adopted to identify non-coding RNAs (ncRNAs) using the Rfam library Rfam.cm.12.2 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Nawrocki EP, Burge SW, Bateman A, Daub J, Eberhardt RY, Eddy SR, Floden EW, Gardner PP, Jones TA, Tate J, et al. Rfam 12.0: updates to the RNA families database. Nucleic Acids Res. 2015;43(Database issue):D1307." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1837">42</a>]. Results with scores lower than the family-specific gathering threshold were removed; when loci on both strands were predicted, only the hit with the highest score was kept. Transfer RNAs were also predicted using tRNAscan-SE [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Schattner P, Brooks AN, Lowe TM: The tRNAscan-SE, snoscan and snoGPS web servers for the detection of tRNAs and snoRNAs. Nucleic Acids Res 2005, 33(Web Server issue):W686W689." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1841">43</a>] at default parameters. Repeat density was calculated from the file that contains the reconciled annotation (Additionalfile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#MOESM10">10</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Analysis of genomic variations</h3><p>Analysis of SNPs and InDels: We used MUMmer (version 3.23) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Kurtz S, Phillippy A, Delcher AL, Smoot M, Shumway M, Antonescu C, Salzberg SLJG. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biol. 2004;5(2):R12." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1855">26</a>] to align the Nipponbare and Zhenshan97 genomes to the KitaakeX genome using parameters -maxmatch -c 90 -l 40. To filter the alignment results, we used the delta -filter 1 parameter with the one-to-one alignment block option. To identify SNPs and InDels we used show-snp option with parameter (Clr TH). We used snpEff [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Cingolani P, Platts A, Wang le L, Coon M, Nguyen T, Wang L, Land SJ, Lu X, Ruden DM. A program for annotating and predicting the effects of single nucleotide polymorphisms, SnpEff: SNPs in the genome of Drosophila melanogaster strain w1118; iso-2; iso-3. Fly. 2012;6(2):8092." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1858">44</a>] to annotate the effects of SNPs and InDels. Distribution of SNPs and InDels along the KitaakeX genome was visualized using Circos [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Krzywinski M, Schein J, Birol I, Connors J, Gascoyne R, Horsman D, Jones SJ, Marra MA. Circos: an information aesthetic for comparative genomics. Genome Res. 2009;19(9):163945." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1861">38</a>].</p><p>Analysis of PAVs and Inversions: We used the show-coords option of MUMmer (version 3.23) with parameters -TrHcl to identify gap regions and PAVs above 86bp in size from the alignment blocks. We used the inverted alignment blocks with 98% identity from the show-coords output file to identify inversions.</p><p>To identify genomic variations between Kitaake and KitaakeX we sequenced and compared the sequences using the established pipeline [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Li G, Jain R, Chern M, Pham NT, Martin JA, Wei T, Schackwitz WS, Lipzen AM, Duong PQ, Jones KC, et al. The sequences of 1504 mutants in the model Rice variety Kitaake facilitate rapid functional genomic studies. Plant Cell. 2017;29(6):121831." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1870">15</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">BAC library construction</h3><p>Arrayed BAC libraries were constructed using established protocols [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Luo M, Wing RA: An Improved Method for Plant BAC Library Construction. In: Plant Functional Genomics. Edited by Grotewold E. Totowa, NJ: Humana Press; 2003: 319." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d1280e1881">45</a>]. Please see Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6262-4#MOESM1">1</a> for further details.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Genome size estimation</h3><p>We used the following methodology to estimate KitaakeX genome size:</p><p>(1) Using the Illumina fragment library, we created a histogram of 24mer frequencies. This was performed by first counting the frequency of all 24mers. The number of kmers at each frequency was tallied, and a histogram was created. (2) The kmer histogram generally indicates a peak value at a particular frequency corresponding to the average coverage of 24mers on the genome. (3) We then took the peak value representing the coverage on the genome, and computed the total bases in the Illumina library. Further dividing the total bases by the coverage, provided an estimate of the genome size. This value is generally accurate to +/10%.</p></div></div></section>
                    <section aria-labelledby="availability-of-data-and-materials" data-title="Availability of data and materials"><div class="c-article-section" id="availability-of-data-and-materials-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="availability-of-data-and-materials">Availability of data and materials</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="availability-of-data-and-materials-content">
              
              <p>The genome sequencing reads and assembly have been deposited under GenBank under accession number PRJNA234782 and PRJNA448171 respectively. The assembly and annotation of the Kitaake genome are available at Phytozome (<a href="https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov/pz/portal.html">https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov/pz/portal.html</a>). The RNA-Seq reads of KitaakeX leaf, panicle, stem and root have been deposited under GenBank accession numbers SRP182736, SRP182738, SRP182741, and SRP182737 respectively. Genome sequencing reads for Kitaake have been deposited under GenBank under accession number SRP193308.</p>

    <a data-track="click" data-track-action="outbound reference" data-track-label="link" aria-label="Search for reference 4 on Google Scholar" href="http://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?&amp;title=A%20draft%20sequence%20of%20the%20rice%20genome%20%28Oryza%20sativa%20L.%20ssp.%20indica%29&amp;journal=Science&amp;volume=296&amp;issue=5565&amp;pages=79-92&amp;publication_year=2002&amp;author=Yu%2CJ&amp;author=Hu%2CS&amp;author=Wang%2CJ&amp;author=Wong%2CGK-S&amp;author=Li%2CS&amp;author=Liu%2CB&amp;author=Deng%2CY&amp;author=Dai%2CL&amp;author=Zhou%2CY&amp;author=Zhang%2CXJ">
